Adults Executing Qisas Before Minors Reach Majority
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qisas timing with minor heirs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether adult heirs may execute qisas before minor heirs reach puberty, state the proof of each side, and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Qisas is indivisible — Abu Hanifa says each heir owns the entire right, so adults may execute immediately.

The question of when qisas can be executed when there are both adult and minor heirs is a classical dispute within the Hanafi school. Abu Hanifa permits immediate execution by the adults, while the Sahibayn require waiting until the minors reach puberty. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position and the evidence that supports Abu Hanifa's view, which is the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Read the passage and locate the specific disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn concerning the timing of qisas when there are minor heirs.
The dispute is about whether adult heirs must wait for minors to reach puberty before executing qisas.
2
State the Sahibayn's proof
Understand why the Sahibayn require waiting: qisas is a shared right, indivisible, and adults lack guardianship over the minors' share.
Their analogy is to a case with one absent adult heir.
3
Examine Abu Hanifa's proof
Analyze his two main arguments: the historical precedent of Ali (tacit ijma) and the legal reasoning that the right is indivisible due to its indivisible cause.
The Companions' lack of objection is treated as consensus.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
See the practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, adult execution is valid; under Sahibayn, it would be wrongful killing. The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.
Also note the sub-cases based on the adult's relationship to the minor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصبي كالمعتوه وللكبار القود قبل كبر الصغار، وإن قتله بمر يقتص إن أصابه في الحكم المذكور أي للأولياء الكبار الحديد، وإلا لا كالخنق والتغريق، لا يقتص هو المذكور في الجامع الصغير؛ لأن المقصود من الصلح المال، والوصي يتولى التصرف فيه كما يتولى الأب، بخلاف القصاص؛ لأن المقصود منه التشفي، وهو مختص بالأب وقالوا: القياس أن لا يملك التصرف في الطرف كما بينهم. لا يملكه في النفس، وفي الاستحسان يملكه؛ لأن الأطراف يسلك بها مسلك الأموال رمز الحقائق: ٤٢٦/٢] وللكبار القود إلخ : يعني إذا كان القصاص مشتركا بأن قتل رجل، وله أولاد كبار وصغار، فللكبار أن يقتلوا القاتل قبل أن يبلغ الصغار، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا ليس لهم ذلك حتى يبلغ الصغار؛ لأن القصاص مشترك تكملة البحر الرائق: ۷۷/۹] ولا يمكن استيفاء البعض؛ لعدم التجزي، ولا الكل؛ لعدم الولاية عليهم، وفيه إبطال حقهم فيؤخر إلى إدراكهم، كما إذا كان بين كبيرين أحدهما غائب. وللإمام ما روي أن عبد الرحمن بن ملجم حين قتل عليا له قتل به، وقد كان في أولاد عليه صغار، ولم ينتظر بلوغهم، وكان ذلك بمحضر من الصحابة من . نكير، فحل محل الإجماع؛ ولأنه حق لا يتجزأ؛ لثبوته بسبب لا يتجزأ، فيثبت لكل كملا كولاية الإنكاح ولهذا لو استوفاه بعض الأولياء، لا يضمن شيئا للقاتل، فلو لم يكن له قتله لضمن كالأجنبي، وكذا لا يضمن للباقين، واحتمال العفو من الصغير منقطع في الحال، بخلاف الكبير الغائب، ولو كان الكبير وليا للصغير ممن يملك التصرف في المال كالأب والجد يستوفيه الكبير قبل بلوغ الصغير بالإجماع، وإن كان وليا للصغير ممن لا يملك التصرف في المال كالأخ والعم، فعلى الخلاف، فإن كان الكبير أجنبيا من الصغير لا يملك الكبير الاستيفاء بالإجماع. (فتح)
And the minor is like the insane, and for the adults there is qisas before the minors reach majority. And if he killed him with a mar, he is retaliated against if he struck him in the mentioned ruling, i.e., for the adult guardians, with the iron, otherwise not, like strangulation and drowning. No qisas. It is mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir; because the purpose of reconciliation is wealth, and the executor manages it as the father manages, contrary to qisas; because its purpose is revenge, and it is specific to the father. And they said: by analogy he cannot dispose of the limb just as he cannot dispose of the life, and by istihsan he can, because limbs are treated like wealth. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/426] And for adults the qisas etc.: meaning if the qisas is shared, i.e., a man was killed and he has adult and minor children, then the adults may kill the killer before the minors reach puberty. This is according to Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: they do not have that right until the minors reach puberty; because qisas is shared. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 9/77] And it is not possible to execute part of it because it is indivisible, nor all of it because there is no guardianship over them, and there is nullification of their right, so it is postponed until they reach maturity, as if there were two adults one of whom is absent. And for the Imam (Abu Hanifa) what is narrated that when Abd al-Rahman ibn Muljam killed Ali (may Allah be pleased with him), he was killed in retaliation, and there were minor children of Ali, and they did not wait for them to reach puberty, and that was in the presence of the companions without disapproval, so it took the place of consensus; and because it is a right that is indivisible due to its being established by an indivisible cause, so it is established for each completely, like the guardianship of marriage. For this reason, if one of the guardians executes it, he does not guarantee anything to the killer; if he did not have the right to kill him, he would guarantee like a stranger. Likewise, he does not guarantee for the rest. And the possibility of pardon by the minor is cut off immediately, unlike the absent adult. And if the adult is a guardian of the minor who manages wealth, like the father and grandfather, the adult executes it before the minority reaches maturity by consensus. And if he is a guardian of the minor who does not manage wealth, like the brother and paternal uncle, then there is the disagreement. And if the adult is a stranger to the minor, the adult does not have the right of execution by consensus. (Fath)
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) concerning the right of adult heirs to execute qisas (retaliatory execution) when there are also minor heirs. The base text states that the minor is like the insane and that adults may execute qisas before the minors reach puberty. The commentator explains this in context: if a man is killed and has both adult and minor children, Abu Hanifa permits the adults to execute the killer immediately, while the Sahibayn say they must wait until the minors reach puberty. The Sahibayn argue that qisas is a shared right, and since it is indivisible, the adults cannot execute part of it (for themselves) nor the whole (because they lack guardianship over the minors' share). Therefore execution should be delayed, similar to a case where one of two adult heirs is absent. Abu Hanifa's proof includes the historical precedent of Imam Ali: when Ali was killed by Ibn Muljam, his minor children did not delay execution, and this happened in the presence of the Companions without disapproval, thus constituting tacit consensus. Additionally, qisas is a right that does not divide because its cause (the murder) is indivisible; thus each heir has full right to it, like the guardianship of marriage. If one heir executes qisas, he does not become liable to the killer (as a stranger would) nor to the other heirs. Moreover, the possibility of the minor pardoning is cut off immediately (the minor cannot pardon before majority), unlike an absent adult whose intention is unknown. The commentator further distinguishes according to the relationship of the adult heir to the minor: if the adult is a father or grandfather (who have authority over the minor's wealth), he can execute by consensus; if he is a brother or uncle (who do not manage wealth), then the disagreement persists; if he is a stranger, he cannot execute by consensus.

Key Terms
القود (al-qawad): Retaliatory execution (qisas) for murder, where the heir of the victim is given the right to have the killer executed.
المعتوه (al-ma'tuh): One who is insane or mentally deficient; similar to a minor in lack of legal capacity.
التشفي (al-tashaffi): Seeking revenge or satisfaction through retaliation; the purpose of qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason Abu Hanifa gives for permitting adult heirs to execute qisas immediately?
A) Adults have guardianship over minors
B) Qisas is divisible among heirs
C) The right of qisas is indivisible due to its indivisible cause, so each heir owns it fully
D) The Sahibayn agree with this view

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa allows adult heirs to execute qisas immediately because the right is indivisible and each heir holds it complete, supported by the precedent of Ali.

Killing with a Mar: When Is Qisas Required?
Ruling on qisas when killing with a mar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four scenarios of killing with a mar and identify which scenarios require qisas and which require diya according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Qisas depends on which part of the killing tool strikes: iron edge, iron back (with/without wound), or wood.

The 'mar' is a digging tool with a long wooden handle and an iron head. The Hanafi school distinguishes between the various parts of the tool—the sharp edge, the blunt back, and the wood—when determining whether qisas (retaliation) is due. This lesson unpacks the four ruling scenarios and the intra-Hanafi dispute over whether a wound is necessary when the back of the iron strikes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three parts of the mar
The mar has three distinct parts: the sharp iron edge (hadd al-hadid), the blunt back of the iron (zahr al-hadid), and the wooden handle (al-ud). The ruling hinges on which part caused death.
2
Learn the ruling for each part
Edge: qisas by consensus. Back with wound: qisas by consensus. Back without wound: disputed (zahir al-riwaya says qisas, al-Tahawi says diya). Wood: no qisas, only diya.
3
Understand the condition of wounding
The dispute centers on whether a wound (jarh) is a condition for qisas when the blunt back strikes. The zahir al-riwaya does not require it; al-Tahawi's narration does.
This dispute was mentioned earlier in the book and is specific to the Hanafi school.
4
Apply the rulings to hypothetical scenarios
Given a scenario, identify which part of the mar struck and whether there was a wound, then determine qisas or diya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قتله بمرّ :إلخ المر بفتح الميم وتشديد الراء، وهو خشبة طويلة في رأسها حديدة عريضة من فوقها خشبة عريضة يضع الرجل رجله عليها، ويحفر بها الأرض يعني إن قتله بمر، فإن أصابه الحد يقتص القاتل، وإلا لا يقتص، والمسألة على أنحاء؛ لأنه إما أن يصيبه بحد الحديد أو بظهره أو بالعود، فإن أصابه بحد الحديد يقتص إجماعا، وإن أصابه بظهر الحديد، فإن جرحه يقتص أيضا بالإجماع، وإن لم يجرحه، فعن الإمام روايتان: في ظاهر الرواية يقتص؛ لأنه لا يشترط الجرح، وعلى رواية الطحاوي يشترط الجرح، فلا يقتص، وقد ذكر الخلاف في اشتراط الجرح أول الكتاب فتذكره، وإن لم يصبه الحديد بل أصابه العود لا يقتص، بل تجب الدية؛ كي لا يهدر الدم. (ملتقطا من الكتب بتوضيح)
And if he killed him with a mar: etc. Al-mar: with fatha on the meem and tashdid on the ra, and it is a long stick with a wide iron at its head, above which is a wide stick on which a man places his foot and digs the earth with it. Meaning if he killed him with a mar, then if the edge struck him, the killer is retaliated against, otherwise not. The issue has several cases: because either he strikes him with the edge of the iron, or the back, or the wood. If he strikes him with the edge of the iron, qisas is due by consensus. If he strikes him with the back of the iron, and it wounds him, qisas is also due by consensus. If it does not wound him, then there are two narrations from the Imam (Abu Hanifa): in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), qisas is due because wounding is not a condition. According to the narration of al-Tahawi, wounding is a condition, so no qisas. The disagreement about the condition of wounding has been mentioned at the beginning of the book, so remember it. And if the iron did not strike him, but the wood struck him, then no qisas, but diya is required so that blood is not wasted. (Multaqit min al-Kutub bi-Tawdih)
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on qisas (retaliation) when a person kills another using a 'mar' — a digging tool consisting of a long wooden handle with a wide iron blade at the top, used for digging (e.g., a spade or pickaxe). The key is the point of impact. There are four scenarios: (1) If the iron edge strikes the victim, qisas is obligatory by unanimous consensus. (2) If the back of the iron strikes and causes a wound, again qisas by consensus. (3) If the back of the iron strikes but does not cause a wound, there is a dispute within the Hanafi school: according to the zahir al-riwaya (the prominent transmission from Abu Hanifa), qisas is due because wounding is not a precondition for qisas; but according to al-Tahawi's narration, wounding is necessary, so no qisas. (4) If the wooden part of the tool strikes (not the iron), then qisas is not due; instead, the killer must pay diya (blood money) to prevent the victim's blood from being wasted. The passage also reminds the reader that the dispute about requiring wounding for qisas has been discussed earlier in the book.

Key Terms
المر (al-mar): A digging tool with a long handle and an iron head, like a spade or pickaxe.
الحد الحديد (hadd al-hadid): The sharp edge of the iron part of the mar.
ظهر الحديد (zahr al-hadid): The back or blunt side of the iron part.
العود (al-ud): The wooden handle or part of the mar.
الجرح (al-jarh): A wound; an external injury that breaks the skin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone is killed by the wooden handle of a mar, what is the ruling?
A) Qisas is due
B) No qisas, but diya is required
C) No compensation at all
D) Diya plus additional penalty

Key Takeaway
The ruling for killing with a mar depends on the part that strikes: iron edge always qisas, iron back with wound qisas, iron back without wound disputed (qisas in zahir al-riwaya), wood only diya.

Strangulation and Drowning: Qisas or Diyah?
Dispute on whether qisas applies in strangulation and drowning deaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on qisas for strangulation and drowning, explain the dispute with the Sahibayn and Shafi'i, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
For Abu Hanifa, strangulation and drowning do NOT trigger qisas—only diya; the execution method reflects the crime type only when qisas applies.

This passage extends a principle from a prior case (wooden parts) to the methods of killing by strangulation and drowning. It presents a clear intra- and inter-madhhab dispute. You will see how the Hanafi school, through Abu Hanifa, limits qisas to direct, weapon-like killing, while the Sahibayn and Shafi'i treat the method of killing as relevant to the applicability and method of qisas.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify Abu Hanifa's ruling: no qisas for strangulation or drowning; only diya is wajib. Note the condition of habituality triggering siyasa.
This is the view relied upon in the school.
2
Identify the opposing views
State that the Sahibayn and Imam al-Shafi'i hold that qisas is required. Note the difference in execution method: Sahibayn say beheading, Shafi'i says drowning.
3
Understand the role of siyasa
Abu Hanifa's position does not leave the habitual offender unpunished; the Imam can execute him as a discretionary policy (siyasa) to prevent future crimes.
This prevents impunity while maintaining the principle that qisas is not due.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the killer pays diya and may be executed by siyasa if habitual; under the opposing views, the killer is executed by beheading or drowning regardless.
This affects the outcome in judicial systems that follow the Hanafi school.
5
Examine the source authority
Note that the text cites al-Asl by Imam Muhammad, indicating that Abu Hanifa's view is part of zahir al-riwayah.
This strengthens the position's weight within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(ملا مسكين بزيادة)
Like strangulation and drowning: meaning qisas is not required for the wood, and diya is required, just as qisas is not required in strangulation and drowning, and diya is required in them according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and according to al-Shafi'i, qisas is required, except that according to the two it is executed by beheading, and according to him (al-Shafi'i) by drowning. He said in al-Asl: If a man strangled another until he died, then according to Abu Hanifa's statement, Allah [knows], no qisas, but if he does that habitually, the Imam may kill him for policy. (Mulla Miskeen with addition)
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage extends the previous ruling about the wooden part of the mar (where no qisas applies) to strangulation and drowning. It presents the Hanafi school's position according to Abu Hanifa: for death by strangulation or drowning, qisas is not applicable; instead, diya (blood money) is wajib. However, this is a point of ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school as well as between the schools. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that qisas is required, but they differ on the method: the Sahibayn say the offender should be executed by beheading (hazzan), while Imam al-Shafi'i holds that qisas is required by drowning (the same method as the crime). The text also mentions that if the offender habitually strangles people, the Imam may execute him as a discretionary punishment (siyasa), even without qisas. This shows that while qisas is not imposed, the state has authority to prevent repeated crimes.

Key Terms
الخنق (al-khanq): Strangling, choking to death by compression of the neck.
التغريق (al-taghriq): Drowning, causing death by submersion in water.
سياسة (siyasa): Discretionary punishment by the ruler for public order, not a fixed hadd or qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling for a person who strangles another to death?
A) Qisas by strangulation
B) Qisas by beheading
C) No qisas, but diya is required
D) No punishment at all

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, killing by strangulation or drowning does not incur qisas under Abu Hanifa's view; only diya is wajib, but the ruler may impose siyasa for habitual offenders.

Qisas for Intentional Wound Leading to Death After Bedridden Period
Ruling that qisas applies when a wound leads to death after a bedridden period

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which qisas applies when a wound leads to death after a period of bedridden illness, and identify the factors that sever the causal link.

Core Idea
An intentional wound causing death – even after a bedridden period – requires qisas unless the victim recovers fully or performs an act indicating non-terminal condition.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq clarifies a crucial point in Hanafi criminal law: what happens if a victim does not die instantly but becomes bedridden and later dies from the wound? The ruling affirms the continuity of causation and sets clear exceptions that break the chain. Understanding this helps distinguish between direct and indirect causation in homicide cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its consequence
The act is intentional wounding (jarh amdan). The consequence is the victim's death after becoming bedridden. Determine if the death is attributable to the wound.
Bedridden (dhu firash) indicates the victim never recovered.
2
Recognize the causal principle
In Hanafi law, a clear cause (sabab zahir) attributes the result to the actor. The wound is the apparent cause of death unless an intervening factor severs the link.
Causation is direct; the wound need not be instantaneous.
3
Note the exceptions
Two factors break causation: full recovery from the wound (then death from another cause), or the victim freeing a slave during illness (indicating expectation of survival).
Freeing a slave is a virtuous act that suggests the victim believed he would recover.
4
Apply the ruling
If none of the exceptions are present, qisas (retaliatory execution) is wajib. The wounder is fully liable for the death.
This ruling is consistent with the Hanafi position on causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن جرح رجلا عمدا فصار ذا فراش ومات يقتص. ومن جرح إلخ: يعني إذا جرح إنسان آخر، فصار المجروح صاحب فراش حتى مات، فإنه يقتص من الجارح؛ لأن الجرح سبب ظاهر لموته، فيحال الموت عليه ما لم يوجد ما يقطعه كحر الرقبة، أو البرء منه. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٧٨/٩]
And whoever wounds a man intentionally so that he becomes bedridden and dies, he is retaliated against. And 'whoever wounds etc.': meaning if a person wounds another, and the wounded becomes bedridden until death, then qisas is executed on the wounder; because the wound is a clear cause of his death, so death is attributed to it unless something cuts it off, like freeing of a slave, or recovery from it. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 9/78]
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage states a clear ruling: if a person intentionally wounds another, and as a result the victim becomes bedridden (i.e., confined to bed due to the injury) and subsequently dies, the wounder is subject to qisas (retaliatory execution). The reasoning provided in the commentary is that the wound is an apparent (zahir) cause of death. In Hanafi law, when there is a clear causal link between the injury and death, responsibility is assigned to the wounder, provided no intervening factor severs that causal connection. The commentary mentions two factors that would break the chain: (1) if the victim frees a slave (which is a virtuous act that might indicate the victim was not terminally ill), or (2) if the victim fully recovers from the wound then later dies from another cause. As long as the death is directly attributable to the wound, qisas is wajib. This ruling is consistent with the general Hanafi principle that causation (sabab) in homicide matters must be clear and direct.

Key Terms
ذا فراش (dhu firash): Bedridden; confined to bed due to illness or injury.
حر الرقبة (harr al-raqaba): Freeing a slave; an act that breaks the causal chain in the context of wounds leading to death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The victim eating after the wound
B) The victim recovering fully from the wound
C) The victim sleeping after the wound
D) The victim speaking after the wound

Key Takeaway
An intentional wound causing death after a bedridden period triggers qisas unless the victim fully recovers or frees a slave, which severs the causal link.

Killing an Armed Attacker in Self-Defense
Ruling on killing an armed attacker, with distinctions for weapon type and location

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on killing an armed attacker in self-defense and apply the distinctions based on weapon type, time, and location to determine whether the killer is liable.

Core Idea
Killing an armed attacker is wajib or mubah, but liability depends on the weapon's lethality and availability of help.

Self-defense is a fundamental right in Islamic law, but it is not unlimited. The Hanafi school sets precise conditions: the attacker must pose a lethal threat, and the defender must have no other recourse. This lesson examines the ruling when someone draws a weapon against you, with a special focus on the distinction between a sword and a stick, and how the presence of help changes the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios and their rulings
The passage covers multiple scenarios: drawing a sword/knife, drawing a weapon generally, and drawing a stick. Note the ruling for each: sword/knife → wajib to kill, no liability; weapon (silah) → no liability; stick at night or outside city → no liability; stick by day in city → liable for qisas.
The stick is the key variable.
2
Locate the textual proof (dalil)
Find the hadith cited: “Whoever draws a sword against the Muslims has made his blood permissible.” Also note the rational proof: repelling harm is obligatory.
The hadith is ahad but its meaning is clear (qati al-dalala).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase “أبطل دمه” (made his blood permissible) directly indicates that killing is lawful and no retaliation or blood money is due. This applies when the attacker draws a sword.
For the stick, the hadith is extended by analogy based on lethality and necessity.
4
Analyze the conditions for the stick
The ruling for the stick depends on time and place: at night or outside the city, help is not available, so killing is permitted. By day in the city, help is expected, so killing is excessive and leads to liability.
This is based on the principle of daf al-darar (repelling harm) only when necessary.
5
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa holds that a stick is not like a weapon, so killing with a stick by day in city is not justified. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) may consider the stick equivalent to a weapon due to its potential to kill, thus allowing killing without liability.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa’s view, as stated in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن شهر على المسلمين سيفا، وجب قتله، أو سكينا ولا شيء بقتله، ومن شهر على رجل سلاحا ليلا أو نهارا في المصر أو غيره، أو شهر عليه عصا ليلا في مصر، أو نهارا في غيره، فقتله المشهور عليه، فلا شيء عليه، الشاهر أو غيره دفعا عنه المشهور عليه. وإن شهر عليه عصا نهارا في مصر، فقتله المشهور عليه قتل به، وإن شهر المجنون على غيره سلاحا فقتله المشهور عليه عمدا، هدر مطلقا أي المجنون هدر مطلقا. ومن جرح إلخ: يعني إذا جرح إنسان آخر، فصار المجروح صاحب فراش حتى مات، فإنه يقتص من الجارح؛ لأن الجرح سبب ظاهر لموته، فيحال الموت عليه ما لم يوجد ما يقطعه كحر الرقبة، أو البرء منه. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٧٨/٩] ضمن زيد إلخ: لأن فعل الأسد والحية جنس واحد؛ لكونه هدرا في الدنيا والآخرة، وفعله بنفسه جنس آخر؛ لكونه هدرا في الدنيا معتبرا في الآخرة، حتى يأثم به، وفعل زيد معتبر في الدنيا والآخرة، فصار ثلاثة أجناس: ومعتبر ومعتبر من وجه دون وجه وهو فعله بنفسه، فيكون الثابت فعلا واحدا، فيجب على زيد ثلث الدية، ثم إن كان فعل زيد عمدا تجب عليه الدية في ماله وإلا فعلى العاقلة تكملة البحر الرائق: ٧٨/٩] ومن شهر إلخ ولا شيء بقتله؛ لقوله عليه: "من شهر على المسلمين سيفا فقد أبطل دمه"، ولأن دفع الضرر واجب، فوجب عليهم قتله إذا لم يمكن دفعه إلا به، ولا يجب على القاتل شيء؛ لأنه صار باغيا بذلك، وكذا إذا أشهر على رجل سلاحا فقتله، أو قتله غيره دفعا، فلا يجب بقتله شيء؛ لما بينا، ولا يختلف بين أن يكون بالليل أو بالنهار، في المصر أو خارج المصر؛ لأنه لا يلحقه الغوث بالليل، ولا في خارج المصر، فكان له دفعه بالقتل. تكملة البحر الرائق: ۷۹/۹] ومن شهر على رجل إلخ : أفاد بهذه المسألة أن الواحد كالمسلمين في الحكم المذكور سابقا، ولما كان السلاح لا يمهل ، فيحتاج إلى دفعه بالقتل ليلا كان أو نهارا في مصر أو غيره. عصا إلخ: سواء كان صغيرة أو كبيرة؛ لأن العصا الصغيرة وإن كانت تمهل، ولكن في الليل لا يلحقه الغوث، فيضطر إلى دفعه بالقتل، وكذا في النهار في غير المصر في الطريق لا يلحقه الغوث، فإذا قتل كان دمه هدرا. فلا شيء عليه: أي على المشهور عليه بقتل الشاهر لما روينا وبينا من المنقول والمعقول، وهذا إذا لم تدل الحال على المزح واللعب، ولا يعتبر احتمال الجد وإظهار المزح، ولو دل الحال على الجد جاز قتله دفعا. (فتح) قتل به أي قتل المشهور عليه بالشاهر؛ لأن العصا ليس كالسلاح، والظاهر لحوق الغوث نهارا في المصر، فكان بالقتل متعديا وهذا عند أبي حنيفة ظاهر؛ لأنه ليس كالسلاح عنده وقيل عندهما يحتمل أن يكون على الخلاف المذكور في شبه العمد ؛ لأنه كالسلاح عندهما حتى يجب القصاص بالقتل به. (عيني، تكملة)
And whoever draws a sword against Muslims, it is obligatory to kill him, or a knife, and there is nothing upon his killer. And whoever draws a weapon against a man, by night or day, in the city or elsewhere, or draws a stick against him by night in the city, or by day elsewhere, and the person against whom it is drawn kills him, then there is nothing upon him, neither the one who drew nor another in defense. And if he draws a stick against him by day in the city, and the person against whom it is drawn kills him, he is killed in retaliation. And if a lunatic draws a weapon against someone, and the person against whom it is drawn kills him intentionally, it is entirely wasted (hader), i.e., the lunatic is entirely wasted. And 'whoever wounds etc.': meaning if a person wounds another, and the wounded becomes bedridden until death, then qisas is executed on the wounder; because the wound is a clear cause of his death, so death is attributed to it unless something cuts it off, like freeing of a slave, or recovery from it. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 9/78] 'Zayd is liable etc.': because the action of the lion and the snake are of one type; since they are negligible in this world and the Hereafter. And his own action is another type; since it is negligible in this world but considered in the Hereafter, such that he sins by it. And Zayd's action is considered in this world and the Hereafter. Thus there are three types: considered, and considered from one perspective but not another (which is his own action). So what is established is one act, so Zayd owes one-third of the diya. Then if Zayd's action was intentional, the diya is due from his own wealth; otherwise it is upon the 'aqila. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 9/78] And 'whoever draws etc.': and nothing upon his killer; because of his statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever draws a sword against the Muslims has made his blood permissible.' And because repelling harm is obligatory, so it became obligatory for them to kill him if it cannot be repelled except by that. And nothing is due from the killer because he becomes a transgressor (baghi) thereby. Similarly, if he draws a weapon against a man and kills him, or another kills him in defense, then nothing is due for his killing; as we have stated. And there is no difference between night and day, in the city or outside the city; because he cannot get help at night, nor outside the city, so he has the right to repel him by killing. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 9/79] And 'whoever draws against a man etc.': This ruling teaches that the individual is like the Muslims in the previously mentioned ruling. Since a weapon does not give respite, there is need to repel by killing, whether by night or day, in the city or elsewhere. 'A stick etc.': whether small or large; because even a small stick gives respite, but at night he cannot get help, so he is compelled to repel by killing. Similarly, during the day outside the city, on the road, he cannot get help. So if he kills, his blood is wasted. 'Nothing upon him': i.e., upon the person against whom it is drawn, who kills the drawer, based on what we have narrated and explained from transmitted and rational proofs. This applies when the circumstances do not indicate jest and play. And the possibility of seriousness with appearance of jest is not considered. If the circumstances indicate seriousness, then it is permissible to kill him in defense. (Fath) 'He is killed by it': i.e., the person against whom the stick is drawn kills the drawer; because a stick is not like a weapon, and the apparent is that help can be reached during the day in the city, so by killing he commits aggression. This is apparent according to Abu Hanifa; because a stick is not like a weapon in his view. And it is said that according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it may be that the disagreement is like the one mentioned in shibh al-amd; because the stick is like a weapon in their view so qisas is obligatory by killing with it. (Ayni, Takmila)
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This extensive passage covers the ruling on killing an armed attacker in self-defense. The base matn states that if someone draws a sword (or knife) against Muslims, it is obligatory to kill him, and his killer incurs no liability. The same applies if a weapon is drawn against an individual: the victim (or another person acting in self-defense) may kill the attacker without liability, whether by night or day, inside or outside the city. However, there is a distinction for a stick (cudgel): if a stick is drawn by night in the city or by day outside the city, killing the attacker is still permitted without liability because help cannot be obtained. But if a stick is drawn by day inside the city, the person attacked may not kill the attacker; if he does, he is subject to qisas (retaliation) because he could have sought help and the stick is not a lethal weapon. Additionally, if a lunatic draws a weapon, killing him yields no liability. The commentary provides the proof from the hadith: 'Whoever draws a sword against the Muslims has made his blood permissible.' It also gives the rational proof: repelling harm is obligatory, and killing is necessary if it is the only way. The key distinction for the stick: by day in the city, help is likely available, so killing is not justified. The text also notes a difference between Abu Hanifa and the two companions: according to Abu Hanifa, a stick is not like a weapon for the purpose of this ruling, so killing with a stick in daytime city is not justified. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, a stick may be considered like a weapon (similar to shibh al-amd), so the ruling might be different (they might allow qisas if killed with a stick). The passage also includes some cross-references to earlier lessons about wounding and multiple causes, but those seem to be editorial insertions.

Key Terms
شهر (shahara): To draw or unsheathe a weapon, especially a sword, in a threatening manner.
سلاح (silah): Weapon; any instrument used for fighting, including swords, knives, etc.
عصا (asa): Stick, cudgel; a less lethal weapon than a sword.
هدر (hadar): Wasted; blood that is not protected by law, i.e., no liability for killing.
باغي (baghi): A transgressor; an unjust aggressor who rebels against legitimate authority or attacks others without right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The victim has no liability, as the attacker forfeited his life
B) The victim is liable for qisas (retaliation)
C) The victim must pay diya
D) The victim is subject to ta'zir punishment

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of killing an attacker in self-defense depends on the weapon, time, and location; a stick by day in the city does not justify killing because help can be sought.

Killing a Stick-Wielder by Day: Abu Hanifah vs. the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qisas for killing a stick-wielder

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Abu Hanifah's and the Sahibayn's positions on qisas for killing a stick-wielder and apply the factors (time, place, weapon type) to determine the ruling.

Core Idea
When help is available, killing a stick-wielder is transgressive – qisas applies per Abu Hanifah.

In the Hanafi school, the evaluation of self-defense homicides depends on whether the victim could have summoned help without resorting to lethal force. This lesson examines a classical dispute between Abu Hanifah and his two leading disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, regarding a scenario where a person brandishes a stick by day in a city and is killed by the intended victim. By analyzing the reasoning behind each position, you will learn the nuanced criteria that determine whether qisas (retributive punishment) is obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Read the passage and note the key variables: the weapon is a stick (not a sword), the time is daytime, the location is a city, and the victim kills the brandisher. Understand that these factors matter differently to each imam.
2
State Abu Hanifah's position and reasoning
Abu Hanifah holds that qisas is wajib because the stick is not like a lethal weapon, and help is available in a city during daytime. Therefore, killing the brandisher was transgressive (tacaddi).
He emphasizes the ability to seek help rather than fight.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and reasoning
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad analogize a stick to a weapon, or consider it quasi-intentional (shibh 'amd). Thus, the killing may be justified self-defense or, at most, require diyah, not qisas.
They focus on the dangerous nature of a stick as a potential deadly instrument.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
The practical consequence: under Abu Hanifah, the killer is executed (qisas); under the Sahibayn, he either goes free (justified defense) or pays blood money (diyah).
This affects court rulings and fatwa practice.
5
Note the tarjih (preferred view)
The commentator (Ayni) says Abu Hanifah's view is zahir (apparent). Later Hanafi fatwa may follow the Sahibayn in some cases, but the relied-upon position for this specific scenario is Abu Hanifah's.
Understand that later consensus may shift; always check mufta bihi in your locality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن شهر عليه عصا نهارا في مصر، فقتله المشهور عليه قتل به. قتل به أي قتل المشهور عليه بالشاهر؛ لأن العصا ليس كالسلاح، والظاهر لحوق الغوث نهارا في المصر، فكان بالقتل متعديا وهذا عند أبي حنيفة ظاهر؛ لأنه ليس كالسلاح عنده وقيل عندهما يحتمل أن يكون على الخلاف المذكور في شبه العمد ؛ لأنه كالسلاح عندهما حتى يجب القصاص بالقتل به. (عيني، تكملة)
But if he brandishes a stick against him by day in the city, and the one upon whom it is brandished kills him, he is killed for it. 'Killed for it' meaning the one upon whom it is brandished is killed for the brandisher, because the stick is not like a weapon, and it is apparent that help can be obtained by day in the city, so the killing was transgressive, and this is apparent according to Abu Hanifah, because it is not like a weapon according to him. And it is said that according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is likely to be on the difference mentioned in quasi-intentional homicide, because it is like a weapon according to them, so qisas is obligatory by killing with it. (Ayni, Completion)
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage addresses the case where someone brandishes a stick (even a large one) at another person by day in a city, and the victim kills the brandisher. According to Abu Hanifah, because help is available in a city during daytime, the killer is deemed a transgressor and is subject to qisas. Abu Hanifah distinguishes between a stick and a weapon: a stick does not have the same lethal potential, so killing the stick-wielder is unjustified when help can be obtained. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) disagree; they analogize a stick to a weapon and hold that killing in self-defense is permitted, and thus no qisas is due—or in the alternative, they classify it as quasi-intentional (shibh 'amd) where diyah applies but not qisas. The text mentions a difference of opinion about whether it falls under shibh al-'amd. The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifah for this specific scenario: qisas is due because the killing was excessive when non-lethal means were available.

Key Terms
شبه العمد (shibh al-'amd): Quasi-intentional homicide; an act that is intentional in the doing but not in the intent to kill, or using a tool that does not normally kill.
شهر (shahara): To brandish, flourish, or draw a weapon/stick in a threatening manner.
عصا ('asa): A stick, staff, or rod; not inherently a lethal weapon in Hanafi jurisprudence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifah, what is the ruling on killing a person who brandishes a stick by day in a city?
A) No liability; it is self-defense
B) Qisas is due because help is available
C) Only diyah is due (shibh 'amd)
D) It is permissible if the stick is large

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, qisas for killing a stick-wielder hinges on whether help was readily available; Abu Hanifah's view (qisas due) is the relied-upon position when daytime and city conditions are met.

Qisas — Retaliation

When Does Self-Defense Become Murder? Hanafi Ruling on Killing an Aggressor Who Withdraws
Ruling on qisas when the initial aggressor withdraws and is then killed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas when an initial aggressor withdraws and is then killed, and explain the legal reasoning (ʿilla) for the restoration of ʿiṣma.

Core Idea
Once the attacker withdraws and ceases to threaten, his inviolability returns; killing him afterwards is murder requiring qisas.

What if someone attacks you, you defend yourself, but then he stops? Can you still kill him? The Hanafi school draws a sharp line: the right to kill in self-defense lasts only as long as the threat persists. This lesson unpacks the ruling of 'qatl al-qātil' — the killing of an aggressor who has withdrawn — and the precise moment at which his life regains its legal inviolability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the specific scenario? The initial aggressor (shāhir) draws a weapon, strikes, and then withdraws. The original victim then kills him. The ruling: qisas is wajib (obligatory) on the killer.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is qiyas (analogy) based on the general principle that blood becomes halal only due to ongoing aggression. Once the cause ceases, the original prohibition returns.
No direct text from Quran or Sunnah is cited; the reasoning is analogical.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the analogy work? The striker's blood was halal while actively attacking because his aggression threatened the victim's life. Withdrawal removes that threat, so the justification for killing ends. The striker returns to his original protected status (ma‘ṣūm).
The key concept is that inviolability (ʿiṣma) is not permanently lost; it is conditionally suspended.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The passage presents the ruling as clear and does not mention disagreement. It is likely muttafaq ‘alayh among the Hanafi school, though other schools may have nuances.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
This ruling is the mufta bihi (operative position) in the Hanafi school, as it is consistent with the principles of self-defense and the protection of life.
Implicitly, the ruling is from the Ẓāhir al-Riwāya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قتل القاتل: معناه إذا شهر رجل على رجل سلاحا فضربه الشاهر ، فانصرف بعد الضرب، ثم إن المضروب المشهور عليه ضرب الضارب، وهو الشاهر، فقتله، فعليه القصاص؛ لأن الشاهر لما انصرف بعد الضرب عاد معصوما مثل ما كان؛ لأن حل دمه كان باعتبار شهره و ضربه، فإذا رجع على وجه لا يريد ضربه ثانيا اندفع شره، فلا حاجة إلى قتله؛ لارتفاع شره بدونه فعادت عصمته، فإذا قتله بعد ذلك فقد قتل رجلا معصوما ظلما، فيجب عليه القصاص
Killing the killer: Its meaning: if a man draws a weapon against another man, and the one who drew it [الشاهر] strikes, then withdraws after the strike, then the one struck (the one against whom the weapon was drawn) strikes the striker (the one who drew) and kills him, then qisas is upon him (the victim-turned-killer). Because when the striker withdrew after the strike, he became protected [معصوما] like he was before; because his blood was made lawful due to his drawing and striking. When he returned in a manner not intending to strike again, his evil was repelled, so there is no need to kill him. Since his evil is removed without killing, his inviolability returned. So if he kills him after that, he has killed a protected man unjustly, so qisas is obligatory upon him.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, explaining the concept of 'qatl al-qātil' (killing the killer) in a specific scenario. The core issue: a person draws a weapon and strikes another. After striking, he withdraws and is no longer threatening. Then the original victim strikes and kills the now-withdrawn striker. The ruling: qisas (retaliatory execution) is obligatory on the victim-turned-killer. The reasoning: while the striker's blood was temporarily halal (permissible to be killed) while he was actively attacking, once he withdraws and ceases to be a threat, his life regains inviolability. His withdrawal indicates his evil is repelled, so there is no justification for killing him. Thus, killing him after that is unjust murder, and qisas is due. This is a key principle in Hanafi criminal law: the right to self-defense is limited to the duration of the threat.

Key Terms
شاهر (shāhir): One who draws or brandishes a weapon (e.g., a sword) against another person.
عصمة (‘iṣma): Inviolability; the legal protection of life. In this context, the striker's 'isma is temporarily suspended due to his aggression but returns upon withdrawal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, if someone draws a weapon and strikes you, but then withdraws and stops, can you pursue and kill him?
A) Yes, because he initiated aggression.
B) No, because his inviolability is restored after withdrawal; doing so would require qisas.
C) Only if you are sure he will attack again.
D) Yes, but you must pay diyah instead of qisas.

Key Takeaway
The right to self-defense ends when the threat ends; killing an aggressor after withdrawal is murder subject to qisas.

Qisas — Retaliation

Qisas on Limbs: The Joint Condition for Hand and Foot
Ruling statement with conditions for limb qisas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the joint condition to determine whether qisas is obligatory for intentional cutting of a hand or foot in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Limb qisas requires cutting from a joint; size irrelevant, equivalence is key.

This lesson focuses on the Hanafi ruling for qisas on limbs. The key principle is that exact physical equivalence (mumāthala) is required, and this is only possible when the amputation is at a joint. We will explore the conditions for hand and foot injuries and see why the size of the hand does not matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its location
Determine if the injury is a cut to the hand or foot and at what specific point the cut was made (e.g., wrist, mid-forearm, ankle, shin).
The joint is defined as wrist, elbow, or shoulder for the hand; ankle, knee, or thigh for the foot.
2
Apply the joint condition
Check if the cut is at a joint. If yes, equivalence is possible; if no, the cut involves bone and equivalence is impossible.
Cutting bone (e.g., between joints) makes exact retaliation impossible.
3
Assess equivalence
If cut at a joint, qisas is wajib. If not at a joint, qisas is not wajib and diyah (compensation) is due.
The Quranic verse (5:45) commands retaliation but only when exact equivalence is possible.
4
Consider size
Note that the size of the offender's hand relative to the victim's hand is irrelevant because the lost benefit (grip) is the same regardless of size.
This is stated explicitly: 'even if the hand of the cutter is larger.'
5
Conclude the ruling
State clearly: If cut from joint → qisas wajib. If cut bone → diyah. Provide the rationale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب القصاص فيما دون النفس يقتص بقطع اليد من المفصل، وإن كان يد القاطع أكبر، وكذا الرجل ومارن الأنف والأذن والعين إن ذهب ضوءها وهي قائمة، ولو قلعها لا، باب القصاص إلخ: لما فرغ من بيان القصاص في النفس شرع في بيان القصاص فيما دون النفس؛ لأن الجزء يتبع الكل. تكملة البحر الرائق: ۸۱/۹] يقتص بقطع اليد إلخ يعني من قطع يد غيره من المفصل أي الرسغ والمرفق والمنكب قطعنا يده منه؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَالْجُرُوحَ قِصَاص (المائدة: (٤٥)، وأنه ينبئ عن المماثلة، فأينما أمكن وجب القصاص فيه، وإلا فلا، وقد أمكن في القطع من المفصل، فلو قطع ما بين الرسغ والمرفق، أو ما بينه وبين المنكب لم يجب القود؛ لأنه كسر العظم، ولا ضابطة له، وقوله: "وإن كانت يد القاطع أكبر" واصل بما قبله، وأشار بذلك إلى أن لا اعتبار لكبر اليد وصغرها، فإن المنفعة الفائتة بالقطع وهي البطش لا تتفاوت. (عيني، فتح) وكذا الرجل أي مثل اليد الرجل إذا قطعت من المفصل بأن قطعت من الكعب أو الركبة أو الفخذ يقطع رجل القاتل؛ لوجود المماثلة المطلوبة في القصاص، وإن لم تقطع . من المفصل بأن كان القطع في بعض الساق أو الفخذ، فلا قصاص؛ لعدم إمكان المماثلة. (فتح) ومارن الأنف: هو ما لان منه وفضل عن القصبة] أي وكذا مارن الأنف إذا قطعه شخص يقطع منه؛ لإمكان المماثلة، ولو قطع من قصبة فلا قصاص، سواء قطع بعض القصبة أو كلها؛ لأنه عظم. والأذن: أي وكذا الأذن إذا قطعها شخص يقطع منه؛ لوجود المماثلة. (عيني، فتح) والعين: أي وكذا العين إذا ضربت وزال ضوءها، وهي قائمة غير منفسخة يقتص بضوئها؛ لإمكان رعاية المماثلة في هذه الصورة، وهو أن يجعل على عينه قطن ،رطب، وتقابل عينه بمرآة محماة، فيذهب ضوءها، وهو بمثل ذلك مأثور جماعة عن الصحابة هام، وبه حكم علي بحضرة الصحابة من غير خلاف؛ لأنه حدث في زمان عثمان، فسأل عن الصحابة، فلم يكن عندهم فيه شيء حتى جاء علي فقضى بذلك، قال بعضهم: يعرف زوال الضوء إذا أخبر رجلان من أهل العلم به وقال ابن مقاتل بأن لا تدمع إذا قوبلت مفتوحة للشمس. (ملتقطا من الكتب) ولو قلعها لا: أي لو قلع العين لا يجب القصاص؛ لعدم إمكان رعاية المماثلة، ولكن تجب الدية، ولا تقتص اليسرى باليمنى واليمنى باليسرى، وإن كان بعين المجني عليه حول لا يضر بصره اقتص منه، وإن كان فيها حول شديد ينقص من البصر، ففيها حكومة عدل، وإن كان الحول الشديد بعين الجاني دون المجني عليه يخير المجني عليه إن شاء اقتص، وإن شاء ضمن نصف الدية كذا في "الخلاصة"، وفي "الدر" عن "المجتبى": فقأ اليمنى ويسرى من الفاقئ ذاهبة اقتص منه وترك أعمى. (عيني، ملا مسكين، فتح)
Chapter of retaliation for less than life. Retaliation is carried out by cutting the hand from the joint, even if the hand of the cutter is larger. Similarly the foot, the cartilage of the nose, the ear, and the eye if its sight is gone while it remains intact (not gouged out). If he gouges it out, no (qisas). (Commentary) The chapter etc.: When he finished explaining qisas for life, he began explaining qisas for less than life because the part follows the whole. (Tatimmah al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) 'Retaliation by cutting the hand etc.': meaning if someone cuts off another's hand from the joint, i.e., wrist, elbow, or shoulder, we cut his hand from it because of Allah's statement: 'And wounds (are subject to) retaliation' (al-Ma'idah 5:45), and it indicates equivalence. So wherever possible, qisas is obligatory; otherwise not. And it is possible when cutting from a joint. If he cut between the wrist and elbow, or between it and the shoulder, qisas is not obligatory because it is breaking bone, and there is no standard for it. And his statement: 'even if the hand of the cutter is larger' is connected to the preceding, indicating that size of the hand is not considered, for the lost benefit (grip) does not differ. (al-'Ayni, al-Fath) Similarly the foot: like the hand, if the foot is cut from the joint, i.e., ankle, knee, or thigh, the foot of the killer is cut due to the required equivalence. If not from the joint, e.g., part of the shin or thigh, no qisas due to impossibility of equivalence. (al-Fath) And the cartilage of the nose (mārin): it is the soft part that protrudes from the bone. So if someone cuts it, it is cut from him for possibility of equivalence. If he cuts from the bone, no qisas whether part or whole because it is bone. And the ear: similarly, if someone cuts it, it is cut from him for equivalence. (al-'Ayni, al-Fath) And the eye: similarly, if the eye is struck and its sight is lost while it is whole (not punctured or deformed), retaliation is applied to its sight by a process: a wet cotton is placed on his eye, and it is faced with a heated mirror so its sight goes. This method is reported from a group of Companions, and Ali (ra) judged by it in the presence of the Companions without dissent. It occurred in the time of Uthman, he asked the Companions but they had no knowledge until Ali gave that judgment. Some said: loss of sight is known if two knowledgeable men report it. Ibn Muqatil said: it is known if the eye does not tear when opened towards the sun. (Compiled from books) 'If he gouges it out, no': i.e., if he gouges out the eye, qisas is not obligatory due to impossibility of exact equivalence, but diyah is due. And the left eye is not retaliated for the right, nor the right for the left. And if the victim's eye has a squint (hawl) that does not harm his vision, retaliation is done from him. If there is severe squint that reduces vision, then there is a government of fair compensation (hukūmat 'adl). And if the severe squint is in the eye of the offender, not the victim, the victim may choose: if he wishes, he may retaliate, or if he wishes, he may claim half the diyah. So in al-Khulāṣah and in al-Durr from al-Mujtabā: if one gouges out the right eye and the left eye of the gouger is already blind, retaliation is done from him and he is left blind. (al-'Ayni, Mulla Miskin, al-Fath)
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This is a chapter heading and ruling statement on qisas for injuries short of death. The text establishes the general principle: qisas for limbs is only applicable when the amputation is from a joint (wrist, elbow, shoulder for hand; ankle, knee, thigh for foot). This is because equivalence (mumāthala) is only possible at joints; cutting elsewhere involves bone breakage with no precise equivalent. The size of the hand is irrelevant because the functional loss (grip) is the same. For the nose, qisas applies only to the soft cartilaginous part (mārin), not the bone. For the ear, full qisas. For the eye, qisas is applied to the sight (not the eyeball) only if the eye remains intact but sight is lost; the method involves a procedure with heated mirror and cotton, reported from Caliph Ali. If the eye is gouged out (enucleated), no qisas because exact equality is impossible; instead, full diyah is due. Further details: left eye is not exchanged for right eye; squint (hawl) conditions affect whether qisas is applied or compensation is substituted. The passage includes reliance on Quranic verse 5:45 as the primary dalil, and on the practice of the Companions.

Key Terms
المفصل (al-mafṣil): A joint of the body; for the hand: wrist, elbow, shoulder; for the foot: ankle, knee, thigh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the condition for qisas to be wajib on the hand?
A) The hand must be cut from the wrist only.
B) The hand must be cut from any joint (wrist, elbow, or shoulder).
C) The hand size must match the offender's hand.
D) The cut must be through bone.

Key Takeaway
Limb qisas requires amputation at a joint; cutting bone makes exact equivalence impossible and qisas is replaced by diyah.

Qisas — Retaliation

Qisas on Organs: Nose, Ear, and Eye
Ruling statement with conditions for organ qisas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rulings for qisas on nose, ear, and eye, and distinguish between cases where qisas is possible and where compensation is due.

Core Idea
Nose qisas only on soft part; ear qisas always; eye qisas applies to sight, not removal.

This lesson covers qisas for organs: nose, ear, and eye. The Hanafi school has specific rules to ensure exact equivalence. For the nose, only the soft cartilaginous part (mārin) is subject to qisas; the bone is not. For the ear, any part can be retaliated. For the eye, qisas is on the sight using a historical procedure, not on the eyeball itself. Special conditions like squint affect the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the organ and injury type
Determine which organ was injured (nose, ear, or eye) and the nature of the injury (cut, gouge, loss of function).
Each organ has unique rules.
2
For nose: distinguish soft part vs bone
If the soft cartilage (mārin) is cut, qisas is wajib. If the bone (qaṣabah) is cut (even partially), no qisas, only diyah.
The bone has no precise equivalent.
3
For ear: qisas always possible
Since the ear lacks bone, any part cut is subject to qisas. Retaliation is by cutting the equivalent part of the offender's ear.
The ear is fully soft tissue.
4
For eye: check intactness and sight
If the eye remains intact (not gouged) but sight is lost, qisas is applied to the offender's sight using a heated mirror and cotton. If the eye is gouged out, no qisas, full diyah.
The procedure is historically transmitted from Caliph Ali.
5
Consider squint conditions
If the victim's squint does not affect vision, qisas is still applied. If squint reduces vision, hukūmat 'adl (fair compensation). If the offender has squint, the victim may choose qisas or half diyah.
Special conditions adjust the outcome.

Source Text

Key Terms
مارن الأنف (mārin al-anf): The soft cartilaginous part of the nose that protrudes from the nasal bone.
حكومة عدل (ḥukūmat 'adl): Fair compensation assessed by valuation of the injury relative to full diyah, used when qisas is not possible but the injury is not covered by a fixed diyah.
القصبة (al-qaṣabah): The nasal bone; the hard part of the nose.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) By gouging out the offender's eye.
B) By using a heated mirror and cotton to destroy the offender's sight.
C) By cutting the offender's eyelid.
D) Qisas is not applied.

Key Takeaway
Nose qisas only on soft part; ear qisas always; eye qisas on sight with specific procedure; gouging leads to diyah.

Qisas — Retaliation

Conditions for Qisas on Limbs and Eyes
States conditions for qisas on limbs and eyes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for qisas on hands, feet, nose, ears, and eyes, and distinguish cases where qisas is wajib from those requiring diyah or hukumat 'adl.

Core Idea
Qisas requires exact similarity; for eyes, it applies only when sight is lost but the eye remains intact.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq details the limits of qisas for body parts less than life. It emphasizes the principle of al-mumathalah (equivalence) and clarifies when retaliation is wajib and when it is replaced by financial compensation. We will explore the conditions for each limb and the reasoning behind the Hanafi positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule for limb qisas
For the hand and foot, qisas is executed by cutting from the joint, regardless of size difference between the offender's and victim's limbs. This ensures exact similarity in the location of the cut.
The joint is defined as the wrist/elbow/shoulder for the hand and ankle/knee/hip for the foot.
2
Examine the exception for the eye
Qisas on the eye is only permissible when the sight is lost but the eye remains intact and not burst. If the eye is plucked out (i.e., removed from the socket), exact similarity is impossible, so qisas is not applied.
The key condition: 'ذهب ضوءها وهي قائمة غير منفسخة' (sight gone, eye standing intact, not burst).
3
Understand the prohibition on cross-limb qisas
The left limb is not taken in retaliation for the right, nor vice versa. This is because they are considered different in legal value and function; qisas must be exact in type and side.
This applies to eyes as well: the left eye is not taken for the right.
4
Analyze the squint cases
If the victim has a mild squint that does not impair vision, qisas is still obligatory. If the squint is severe and reduces vision, only hukumat 'adl (fair compensation) is due. If the offender has a severe squint (but the victim has normal vision), the victim may choose between qisas or half the diyah.
The reasoning: in the first case, the squint does not affect the equality of retaliation; in the second, the defect in the victim's eye makes exact retaliation impossible; in the third, the offender's defect might cause additional harm, so the victim gets an option.
5
Review the special case from al-Durr
If someone gouges out both eyes of a sighted person and the gouger also loses his own sight, qisas is nevertheless exacted. This is based on a ruling of Caliph Ali (RA) that was approved by the Companions without dissent.
The ruling shows that qisas is not waived simply because the offender becomes blind; what matters is the condition at the time of the injury.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب القصاص فيما دون النفس: يقتص بقطع اليد من المفصل، وإن كانت يد القاطع أكبر، وكذا الرجل ومارن الأنف والأذن والعين إن ذهب ضوءها وهي قائمة غير منفسخة، ولو قلعها لا. ولا تقتص اليسرى باليمنى ولا اليمنى باليسرى، وإن كان بعين المجني عليه حول لا يضر بصره اقتص منه، وإن كان فيها حول شديد ينقص من البصر ففيها حكومة عدل، وإن كان الحول الشديد بعين الجاني دون المجني عليه يخير المجني عليه إن شاء اقتص وإن شاء ضمن نصف الدية. وفي "الدر" عن "المجتبى": فقأ اليمنى ويسرى من الفاقئ ذاهبة اقتص منه وترك أعمى.
Chapter of Retaliation for What is Less Than Life: Retaliation is exacted by cutting the hand from the joint, even if the hand of the cutter is larger, and likewise the foot and the soft part of the nose and the ear and the eye if its light is gone and it is intact not burst, but if he plucks it out, no. And the left is not retaliated with the right nor the right with the left. And if there is in the eye of the victim a squint that does not harm his eyesight, retaliation is exacted from him. And if there is a severe squint that reduces eyesight, then there is a fair compensation. And if the severe squint is in the eye of the offender without the victim, the victim may choose: if he wishes he may retaliate, and if he wishes he may take half the blood-money. And in al-Durr from al-Mujtaba: One who gouged out the right and left of the gouger while they were sighted, retaliation is exacted from him and he is left blind.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof from the commentator. It opens the chapter on qisas for injuries less than homicide. The ruling: For cutting a hand, qisas is done by cutting the offender's hand from the joint (wrist, elbow, or shoulder), even if the offender's hand is larger than the victim's. The same applies to the foot (from ankle, knee, or hip). For the nose, qisas applies only to the soft part (marin), not the bone. For the ear, retaliation is exacted. For the eye, qisas is only when the sight is destroyed but the eye remains intact and not burst; if the eye is plucked out, no qisas (only blood-money). Additional rules: The left eye is not taken for the right, nor vice versa (they differ in value). If the victim has a mild squint that doesn't affect vision, qisas is done. If the squint severely reduces vision, only fair compensation (hukumat 'adl) is due. If the offender has a severe squint but the victim doesn't, the victim may choose between qisas or half the blood-money. A specific case: If someone gouges out both eyes of another who then becomes blind, and the gouger also loses his eyesight, retaliation is exacted and the gouger is left blind—this was the ruling of Ali (RA) during the caliphate of Uthman (RA), approved by the Companions without dissent.

Key Terms
المفصل (al-mafsil): The joint of the limb (wrist, elbow, shoulder for hand; ankle, knee, hip for foot).
مارن الأنف (marin al-anf): The soft, fleshy part of the nose above the bone (the tip).
حكومة عدل (hukumat 'adl): Fair compensation assessed by two trustworthy valuators when exact qisas is impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi madhhab, when is qisas wajib for an eye injury?
A) Whenever the eye is injured, regardless of condition.
B) Only when the eye is plucked out.
C) When the sight is lost but the eye remains intact and not burst.
D) Only if both eyes are injured.

Key Takeaway
Qisas on limbs is from the joint; on eyes only when sight is lost but eye intact; left and right are not interchangeable; squint conditions affect the ruling.

Qisas for Tooth and Wounds with Exact Similarity
Ruling on tooth retaliation and general principle for wounds

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas for a tooth (even if it differs), identify which head wounds permit qisas, and explain the general principle that no qisas applies to bone except the tooth.

Core Idea
Qisas applies to tooth and to wounds where exact equality can be achieved; bone is excluded because exact healing cannot be matched.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq presents the Hanafi position on qisas for teeth and head wounds. The text encapsulates three key rulings: the tooth is subject to qisas despite physical differences; any head wound that allows for exact similarity (like al-mudihah) also requires qisas; and no bone is eligible for qisas except the tooth. These rulings are grounded in the Quranic verse on qisas and the Hanafi principle of exact similarity (mumathala). Let us unpack each ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the first ruling: tooth qisas even if they differ
Identify that the text says 'والسن وإن تفاوتا' — the tooth is subject to qisas even if the offender's tooth differs in size, whiteness, or other qualities. The basis is the Quranic verse 'والسن بالسن' interpreted as equality in essence despite external differences.
The benefit (chewing) is the same, so exact similarity is not required in external appearance.
2
Identify the second ruling: head wounds with exact similarity
The text says 'وكل شجة يتحقق فيها المماثلة كالموضحة' — any head wound (shajjah) where exact similarity can be achieved, like al-mudihah (which exposes bone), requires qisas. The principle is that if the wound can be measured precisely, retaliation is wajib.
Al-mudihah is the only type mentioned, but other wounds with exact similarity are included.
3
Understand the third ruling: no qisas on bone except tooth
The text says 'ولا قصاص في عظم غير السن' — no qisas applies to bone except the tooth. The reason: bone heals unevenly, making exact equality impossible. The tooth is excepted because some argue it is not true bone, and even if it is, filing can achieve exact measurement.
This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on exact similarity (mumathala) as a condition for qisas.
4
Recognize the underlying principle: exact similarity
All three rulings revolve around the principle that qisas is only valid where exact retaliation is possible without adding or diminishing. This principle is derived from the Quranic verse and the Hanafi legal maxim that qisas requires precise equivalence.
Where exact similarity is impossible, the penalty shifts to diyah (blood money).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسن وإن تفاوتا. وكل شجة يتحقق فيها المماثلة كالموضحة. ولا قصاص في عظم غير السن.
And the tooth even if they differ. And every head wound in which exact similarity is possible, like al-mudihah. And no retaliation in bone except the tooth.
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage is a series of rulings with proof. First ruling: Qisas is wajib for a tooth even if the offender's tooth differs from the victim's in size, whiteness, or other qualities, because the benefit (chewing) is the same. Some scholars say the offender's tooth should be filed down to the gum rather than extracted, as extraction may harm the throat, but the relied-upon position is that extraction is allowed, with filing being precautionary only. The qisas applies to the original tooth, not a supernumerary tooth; for that, fair compensation (hukumat 'adl) is paid. Second ruling: For any head wound (shajjah) where exact similarity is possible—such as al-mudihah, which lays bare the bone—qisas is wajib. The basis is the verse 'And wounds are subject to retaliation' (al-Ma'idah 5:45). Third ruling: There is no qisas on any bone except the tooth. The reason: qisas requires exact equality, which is impossible for bone because it heals unevenly; however, the tooth is excepted because doctors differ whether it is truly bone—some say it is a dry nerve tip—and even if it is bone, exact similarity is possible by filing to the exact measure. This is the Hanafi position; other schools may differ on the tooth.

Key Terms
الموضحة (al-mudihah): A head wound that lays bare the bone.
شجة (shajjah): A wound on the head or face that reaches the bone.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, what is the ruling on qisas for a tooth that is different in size from the victim's tooth?
A) Qisas is not allowed because of the difference.
B) Qisas is allowed only if the offender's tooth is filed down.
C) Qisas is allowed because the benefit is the same.
D) Only diyah is payable.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires qisas for teeth and for wounds where exact healing can match, and no retaliation on bone except the tooth.

No Qisas in Limbs Between Different Categories
Inter-madhhab dispute on qisas in limbs between different categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on qisas in limbs between different categories, explain the Hanafi proof based on limbs as property, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Limbs are like property, not lives — exact value similarity is required for qisas and is absent between different categories.

This passage presents a well-known dispute between the Hanafi school and the majority (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) on whether qisas applies to limbs when the parties differ in gender or legal status. The Hanafis hold that limbs are analogous to property, where exact equality is necessary, while the Shafi'is analogize limbs to lives, where qisas applies despite value differences. Understanding this dispute illuminates the Hanafi methodology of 'illah (effective cause) and the importance of precise legal analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed cases
The passage rules on three scenarios: man vs woman, free vs slave, two slaves. Note that qisas is denied in all by the Hanafis.
2
State the Shafi'i principle and its implication
Shafi'i holds that wherever qisas applies in life, it applies in limbs because limbs are subsidiary to lives. This would make qisas wajib in all these cases.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof by qiyas
Hanafis analogize limbs to property (amwal), where similarity in value is a condition. Since value differs by gender and freedom status, qisas is invalid.
4
Understand the rebuttal to the counter-argument
The opponent may argue that two slaves can have equal market value; the Hanafi response is that exact legal similarity is not established by estimation (taqwim), as in property cases.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafi only diyah or 'arsh; Shafi'i also allows qisas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطرفي رجل وامرأة وحر وعبد وعبدين. وقال الشافعي يجب القصاص في جميع ذلك. وأصله: أن كل موضع جرى القصاص فيه بين الأنفس يجري في الأطراف، وما لا فلا؛ لأنها تابعة للأنفس. وبه قال مالك وأحمد حتى لو قطع عبد يد عبد عمدا، أو حر يد حرة عمدا، أو قطع رجل يد امرأة، ففيه القصاص. ولنا أن الأطراف يسلك بها مسلك الأموال، فكانت المماثلة فيها شرطا. ولا مماثلة بين طرفي الذكر والأنثى؛ للتفاوت بينهما في القيمة بتقسيم الشارع، ولا بين الحر والعبد، ولا بين العبدين؛ للتفاوت في القيمة، وإن تساويا فيها بالظن، فصار شبهة منع. فإن قيل: إن استقام عدم المساواة في الحر والعبد لم يستقم بين العبدين؛ لإمكان تساوي قيمتهما بتقويم المقومين، أجيب بأن التساوي إنما يكون بالحرز والظن والمماثلة المشروطة شرعا لا تثبت بذلك كالمماثلة بين الأموال.
And between the limbs of a man and a woman, and between a free man and a slave, and between two slaves (no qisas). And al-Shafi'i said: Qisas is obligatory in all of that. And his principle: wherever qisas applies between lives, it applies to limbs, and where not, not; because limbs are subsidiary to lives. And Malik and Ahmad said the same, such that if a slave cuts the hand of a slave intentionally, or a free man cuts the hand of a free woman intentionally, or a man cuts the hand of a woman, there is qisas in it. And our proof: Limbs are treated like property, so similarity is a condition in them. And there is no similarity between the limbs of a male and a female due to the difference in value by the division of the Lawgiver, nor between a free man and a slave, nor between two slaves due to difference in value, even if they are estimated equal by conjecture, so it becomes a doubt preventing (qisas). If it is said: If the lack of equality is consistent in the free and slave, it is not consistent between two slaves because equality of their values is possible by appraisal of valuers, the answer is: Equality is only by estimation and conjecture, and the similarity required by law is not established by that, like similarity between properties.
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute. Imam al-Shafi'i (and Malik and Ahmad) hold that qisas applies in limbs between a man and a woman, a free person and a slave, and between two slaves, based on the principle that limbs follow the same rules as lives: since a free man is killed for a free woman, and a slave for a slave in life, the same applies to limbs. The Hanafis (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) argue that limbs are treated like property (amwal) not like lives, so exact similarity in value is a condition. The Lawgiver has assigned different values to male and female limbs (e.g., diyah of woman half of man in some cases), so there is no exact similarity. Between free and slave, the value differs. Between two slaves, even if their market values might be equal by estimation, the required exact similarity is not established by conjecture—just as in property, two items of equal price are not considered exactly identical for qisas. Therefore, no qisas; instead, diyah or 'arsh is paid. This is the relied-upon Hanafi view.

Key Terms
شبهة منع (shubhat man'): A doubt that prevents the application of qisas, arising from the probability of inequality.
تقويم المقومين (taqwim al-muqawwimin): The valuation by appraisers to determine market value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi madhhab reject qisas between the limbs of a man and a woman?
A) Because the diyah of a woman is half that of a man.
B) Because limbs follow lives, and a woman's life is worth half.
C) Because the Lawgiver assigned different values to their limbs, preventing exact similarity.
D) Because qisas is only allowed between free men.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, qisas in limbs requires exact equality of value; between parties of different gender or freedom status, such equality is absent, so only diyah or 'arsh is due.

Qisas — Retaliation
