Eight Scenarios for Cutting Then Killing: Merger of Penalties
Detailed exposition of eight scenarios for cutting then killing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the eight scenarios for cutting then killing, identify which cases result in merger of penalties by consensus and which are disputed, and apply Abu Hanifa's rule of non-merger for two intentional acts without recovery.

Core Idea
Merger of penalties is the default, but prevented by recovery or difference in legal classification.

Having covered a single offense, the text now tackles multiple offenses — specifically, when someone cuts a hand and then kills the same person. The ruling depends on two variables: whether the acts were intentional or accidental, and whether the victim recovered from the cut before the killing. This produces eight distinct scenarios. In most, the penalties do not merge by agreement. The key dispute, between Abu Hanifa and his two foremost students, concerns the case where both acts are intentional and there is no recovery. Understanding this dispute illuminates the Hanafi principle of tadakhul (merger) in penal law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Variables
Recognize that the cut and killing can each be either intentional (amd) or accidental (khata'), and there may or may not be recovery (bar') between them. These two binary variables generate 4 × 2 = 8 scenarios.
Recovery means the wound healed before death; the cut's effect was complete.
2
Enumerate the Eight Scenarios Systematically
Start with intention pairs (four): both intentional; both accidental; cut intentional & killing accidental; cut accidental & killing intentional. Then multiply by recovery (yes or no) to get eight. The text lists the outcomes for each.
3
Identify the Three Outcome Categories
The text states: six cases where penalties do NOT merge by agreement; one where they merge by agreement (both accidental, no recovery); one which is disputed (both intentional, no recovery). Locate these in your enumeration.
The consensus non-merger cases include all four with recovery plus the two mixed-intention cases without recovery.
4
Analyze the Disputed Case
For both intentional acts without recovery, Abu Hanifa says penalties do NOT merge (guardian may cut and kill, or just kill); the Sahibayn say they merge (only qisas for killing). Understand the core reason: Abu Hanifa treats the cut as establishing an independent right, while the Sahibayn apply the default principle of tadakhul.
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the assailant may be amputated before execution.
5
Apply the Principle of Tadakhul
The commentary explains that the default in punishments is merger (tadakhul). Merger is prevented by: (a) difference in the legal classification of the acts (one intentional, one accidental) or (b) recovery, which fixes the first act's penalty independently. Memorize this rule.
This principle also applies to the example of multiple strikes: if no recovery, only one diyah is due by consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في تعدد الجناية، ولما فرغ من ذكر حكم الجناية الواحدة شرع في ذكر الجنايات المتعددة؛ لأن الاثنين بعد الواحد تكملة البحر الرائق: ۱۰۳/۹] ومن قطع يد رجل إلخ يعني إذا قطع يد رجل ثم قتله، يجب عليه موجب القطع، وموجب القطع سواء كانا عمدين أو أحدهما عمد والآخر خطأ، أو كانا خطئين وتخلل بينهما برء أو لا، إلا إذا كانا خطئين ولم يتخلل بينهما برء، فهذا ثمانية أوجه؛ لأن القطع إما عمدا أو خطأ، والقتل كذلك إما عمدا أو خطاء، فصار أربعة، ثم إما أن يكون بينهما برء أو لا، فصار ثمانية، ثم في ستة منها لا يتداخلان اتفاقا، وفي واحد وهو ما إذا كانا عمدين و لم يتخلل البرء بينهما لا يتداخلان عنده، ويتداخلان عندهما، وفي واحدة وهو ما إذا كانا خطئين و لم يتخلل بينهما برء، يتداخلان اتفاقا. وتفصيله: أن القطع والقتل لا يخلو من أن يتخلل بينهما برء أو لا، فإن تخلل يعتبر كل فعل ويؤخذ موجبها؛ لأن موجب الأول تقرر بالبرء، فلا يدخل أحدهما في الآخر حتى لو كانا عمدين فللمولى القطع والقتل، ولو خطأين يجب دية ونصف دية، ولو القطع عمدا والقتل خطأ ففي اليد القود، وفي النفس الدية، ولو بالعكس ففي اليد نصف الدية، وفي النفس القود، وإن لم يتخلل برء فلو أحدهما عمدا، والآخر خطأ، اعتبر كل على حدة، ففي الخطأ الدية، وفي العمد القود، فهذه ست صور لا يتداخلان فيها اتفاقا ولو خطأين فالكل جناية واحدة اتفاقا، فتحب دية واحدة، ولو عمدين فعندهما يقتل ولا يقطع وعنده: إن شاء الولي قطع وقتل، وإن شاء قتل. (هذا ما لخصته من الكتب فاحفظه) أخذ بالأمرين إلخ: اعلم أن الأصل في العقوبات التداخل ويجعل الآخر منهما للأول، ويجعل الكل قتلا واحدا إلا أن لا يمكن الجمع باختلاف حكم الفعلين كما إذا كان القطع خطأ، والقتل عمدا، أو على العكس، أو لا يمكن الجمع بتخلل البرء؛ لأن بتخلل البرء ينتهي الفعل الأول بانتهاء أثره، فلا يمكن جعل الثاني متمما للأول. وأما عند عدم تخلل البرء وعند اتحاد حكمها فإن كانا خطئين، يمكن جعل الثاني متمما للأول بالاتفاق ببقاء أثره وتجانس الفعلين، وإن كانا عمدين فعندهما يمكن وعنده : لا ، وقد مر حكم المسألة الخلافية. (فتح بتغيير) إلا في خطئين إلخ استثناء من قوله: "أخذ بالأمرين، فلا يؤخذ فيه إلا بدية واحدة لا غير؛ لأن دية القطع إنما تجب عند استحكام أثر الفعل، وهو أن يعلم عدم السراية والفرق بين هذه الصورة وبين عمدين لا برء بينهما عند أن الدية مثل غير معقول، فالأصل عدم وجوبها، بخلاف القصاص، فإنه مثل معقول. (عيني، فتح) كمن ضربه أي كما يجب دية واحدة في من ضربه مائة. فبرئ إلخ : فإنه يجب فيه دية واحدة؛ لأن الضربات التي برئ منها، ولم يبق لها أثره، سقط أرشها لزوال الشين، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة، وعند أبي يوسف: فيها حكومة محمد: أنه وعن يجب فيها أجرة الطبيب ومن الأدوية، ولو بقي لها أثر بعد البرئ يجب موجبه مع دية النفس بالإجماع؛ لأن الأرش يجب باعتبار الشين في النفس، وهو ببقاء الأثر. [رمز الحقائق: ٤٣٢/٢]
Chapter: On Multiple Offenses. Having completed the ruling for a single offense, he begins discussion of multiple offenses because two after one is a completion. (From al-Bahr al-Raiq: 9/103) 'And whoever cuts a man's hand then kills him' means that if someone cuts a man's hand then kills him, he is liable for the penalty of the cut and the penalty of the kill, whether both are intentional, or one intentional and the other accidental, or both accidental with recovery between them or not, except when both are accidental and there is no recovery between them. So these are eight cases: the cut is either intentional or accidental, and the killing likewise either intentional or accidental, making four. Then either there is recovery between them or not, making eight. In six of these they do not merge by agreement. In one case, which is when both are intentional and there is no recovery between them, according to (Abu Hanifa) they do not merge, but according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) they merge. In one case, which is when both are accidental and there is no recovery between them, they merge by agreement. The detail: the cut and killing are either with recovery between them or without. If recovery occurs, each act is considered and its due is taken, because the due of the first is established by recovery, so one does not enter into the other. Thus if both are intentional, the guardian has (the right to) cutting and killing; if both accidental, a full diyah and a half diyah are due; if the cut is intentional and the killing accidental, for the hand there is qisas and for the life diyah; if the opposite, for the hand half diyah and for the life qisas. If there is no recovery and one is intentional and the other accidental, each is considered separately: for the accidental diyah, for the intentional qisas. These are six cases where they do not merge by agreement. If both are accidental, the whole is one offense by agreement, so only one diyah is due. If both are intentional, according to the two (Sahibayn) he is killed and not cut; according to him (Abu Hanifa): if the guardian wishes, he may cut and kill; if he wishes, he may kill. (This is what I summarized from the books, so memorize it.) 'He is held for both' etc.: Know that the principle in punishments is merger, and the second is made part of the first, making the whole one killing, unless combination is impossible due to a difference in the ruling of the two acts, such as when the cut is accidental and the killing intentional or vice versa, or combination is impossible due to recovery, because with recovery the first act ends with its effect. Regarding when there is no recovery and the rulings are the same: if both are accidental, it is possible to make the second completing the first by agreement because its effect remains and the acts are homogeneous; if both are intentional, according to the two it is possible, but according to him (Abu Hanifa) it is not, and the ruling of the disputed case has passed. (From Fath, with modification) 'Except in two accidental acts without recovery' is an exception from his saying 'he is held for both', so only one diyah is taken, nothing else, because the diyah for the cut is only due when the effect of the act is established, which is when it is known that there is no fatality. The difference between this case and two intentional acts without recovery is that diyah is an irrational equivalent, so the principle is that it is not due, unlike qisas, which is a rational equivalent. (Ayni, Fath) 'Like one who strikes him' i.e., just as one diyah is due in the case of someone who strikes him a hundred times. 'Then he recovered' etc.: one diyah is due because the strikes from which he recovered and left no effect have their compensation waived due to removal of the disfigurement. This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to Abu Yusuf, there is a government assessment (hukuma); according to Muhammad, the wages of the physician and the cost of medicines are due. If an effect remains after recovery, its due is imposed along with the diyah of the life by consensus, because compensation is due for disfigurement in the life, and it remains with the effect. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/432)
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage is an expanded commentary on the ruling for multiple offenses, specifically when someone cuts a person's hand and then kills him. The author presents eight scenarios based on the intention (intentional/accidental) and whether recovery (bar') occurs between the two acts. In six scenarios, there is consensus that the penalties do not merge. In one scenario where both acts are accidental without recovery, they merge by consensus, and only one diyah is due. The disputed scenario is when both acts are intentional without recovery: Abu Hanifa holds that the penalties do not merge, giving the guardian the option to exact both qisas for the cut and qisas for the killing, or only qisas for the killing. The Sahibayn hold that they merge, so only qisas for the killing is due. The commentary explains that the default principle is merger (tadakhul) unless prevented by a difference in the legal classification of the acts (one intentional, one accidental) or by recovery, which establishes the first act's penalty as independent. The example of a person struck a hundred times illustrates when multiple strikes are treated as a single offense because no recovery occurred between them. The intra-Hanafi disagreement is highlighted, and the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's view as per the text's indication.

Key Terms
تداخل (tadakhul): The merger of penalties for multiple offenses into a single penalty.
برء (bar'): Recovery from the injury of the first act before the second act, establishing the first act's penalty independently.
قصاص (qisas): Retaliatory punishment equivalent to the injury, applied to intentional offenses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where someone intentionally cuts a man's hand and then intentionally kills him without recovery, what is Abu Hanifa's ruling regarding the penalties?
A) Only qisas for the killing is due; the cut penalty is merged.
B) The guardian may choose to exact qisas for both the cut and the killing, or only for the killing.
C) Only diyah for the hand is due along with qisas for the killing.
D) No penalty is due because the victim died.

Key Takeaway
In multiple offenses, the default principle of tadakhul is overridden by recovery (bar') or a difference in the legal classification of the acts, and for two intentional acts without recovery, the Hanafi mufta bihi (Abu Hanifa) gives the guardian the choice to exact both qisas or only the killing.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Qisas and Death from Retaliatory Amputation
Commentary on rulings for qisas and death from retaliatory amputation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on liability when a retaliatory amputation leads to death, and apply the principle of shubhah to determine the outcome.

Core Idea
The right to qisas for the limb does not extinguish the right to qisas for the soul.

This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq explores a nuanced scenario: a person cuts another's hand, the victim retaliates by cutting the aggressor's hand, but then the victim dies from the original injury. What happens? The Hanafi school is divided: Abu Hanifa holds that the aggressor is killed for the death, while the two Sahibayn and al-Shafi'i disagree. A further sub-case involves the guardian cutting the killer's hand and then forgiving—again, a split. The key is the distinction between the right to the limb and the right to the soul, and the role of shubhah (ambiguity) in mitigating the penalty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios
The text discusses two main scenarios: (1) the original victim dies from the retaliator's injury, and (2) the aggressor dies from the qisas cut. Additionally, a sub-case: the guardian cuts the killer's hand and then forgives.
Focus on the legal consequence: qisas vs diyah.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
In the first scenario, Abu Hanifa says the aggressor is killed for the victim's death because the right to qisas for the soul is separate. In the second scenario, if the aggressor dies, diyah is on the victim's 'aqlah. In the sub-case, the guardian is liable for diyah of the hand.
Note the use of 'shubhah' to convert qisas to diyah in the guardian case.
3
State the Sahibayn's and al-Shafi'i's position
Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Shafi'i hold that executing the limb qisas discharges all rights, so no further liability arises in either scenario. In the sub-case, the guardian executed his right, so no liability.
Their dalil: the act of cutting indicates full discharge.
4
Analyze the proofs
Abu Hanifa's argument: the right is specifically to kill, not to cut; cutting is not the full right, so it is not a complete execution, leading to diyah via shubhah. The Sahibayn argue that cutting is the right itself, so no liability.
This is a qiyas jali: analogy would require qisas but it is dropped due to shubhah.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practically, under Abu Hanifa, the aggressor is killed if the victim dies; under the Sahibayn, no killing. In the sub-case, the guardian pays diyah under Abu Hanifa; under the Sahibayn, nothing.
The text does not specify which is mufta bihi, but Abu Hanifa's is the zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text

ولو قطع يده إلخ: يعني ولو قطع رجل يد رجل، فاقتص له، فمات المقطوع الأول قبل المقطوع الثاني، قتل المقطوع الثاني به أي بسريان القطع؛ لأنه تبين أن الجناية كانت قتلا عمدا، وحق المقتص له في القصاص في النفس، واستيفاء القطع لا يوجب سقوط حقه في القتل، وعن أبي يوسف أنه يسقط حقه في القصاص؛ لأن إقدامه على القطع دليل على أنه أبرأه من غيره ولو مات المقتص منه، وهو المقطوع قصاصا من القطع، فديته على عاقلة المقتص له عند أبي حنيفة حاله، وقالا : لا شيء عليه؛ لأنه استوفى حقه وهو القطع، فسقط حكم سرايته، وبه قال الشافعي وله أن حقه في القطع والموجود قتل، فلم يكن مستوفيا حقه، فيضمن، وكان القياس أن يجب القصاص إلا أنه سقط للشبهة، فوجبت الدية. [رمز الحقائق: ٤٣٤/٢]
And if he cut his hand and he retaliated against him, and the first died, he is killed for it, and if he cut the hand of the killer and forgave, the cutter is liable for the blood money of the hand.
And if he cut his hand etc.: meaning that if a man cut the hand of another man, and he retaliated against him, and the first victim (the one whose hand was cut) died before the second (the aggressor), the second is killed for it, i.e., due to the spreading of the cutting; because it becomes clear that the injury was intentional killing, and the right of the one who retaliated is to retaliation for the soul, and executing the cutting does not cause the forfeiture of his right to kill. And from Abu Yusuf: that his right to retaliation is forfeited; because his proceeding to cut is evidence that he discharged him from other than it. And if the one against whom retaliation is executed dies, i.e., the one cut as retaliation from the cutting, his blood money is on the 'aqlah of the one who retaliated according to Abu Hanifa in his current state. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: nothing is on him; because he executed his right which is the cutting, so the ruling of its spreading is dropped. And al-Shafi'i said similarly. And for him (Abu Hanifa) that his right is in the cutting, but what occurred is killing, so he did not execute his right, so he is liable. And the analogy would require that qisas be obligatory, but it is dropped due to ambiguity, so blood money becomes obligatory. 'a hundred poor'? ... the cutter is liable etc.: i.e., the cutter, who is the guardian, is liable for the blood money of the hand, i.e., the hand of the killer according to Abu Hanifa, whether qisas was decreed for him or not. And the two said: nothing is on him; because he executed his right, so he is not liable for it; because he deserved to destroy the soul with all its parts. For this, if he did not forgive, he would not be liable, and likewise if it spreads and does not heal, or if he forgave and it did not spread, or if he cut his hand then cut his neck before healing or after. And for him (Abu Hanifa) that he executed what was not his right; because his right is to kill, and this is cutting and severance, and analogy would require that qisas be obligatory but it is dropped due to ambiguity; since he had the right to destroy the limb as a subsidiary of the soul, and when retaliation is dropped, blood money becomes obligatory. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 2/434]
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where one person (A) cuts another's (B) hand. B retaliates by executing qisas on A's hand, but then B dies from the wound. According to Abu Hanifa, A is killed for B's death because the initial injury was intentional and death resulted. However, if A dies from the retaliatory cut, Abu Hanifa says the diyah is on B's 'aqlah, while the Sahibayn and al-Shafi'i hold that nothing is due because B executed his right. A further sub-case: if the guardian of the deceased (B) cuts the killer's hand (A's hand) and then forgives, Abu Hanifa says the guardian is liable for the diyah of the hand, while the Sahibayn say no liability because the guardian had the right to destroy the entire body. The commentator concludes that the analogy would require qisas but it is dropped due to ambiguity, leading to diyah.

Key Terms
اقتص (iqtaṣa): To retaliate, i.e., to exact qisas by inflicting the same injury.
ولي (walī): Legal guardian, specifically the heir entitled to demand qisas or receive diyah for the deceased.
شبهة (shubhah): Ambiguity or resemblance that prevents the full application of qisas, leading to diyah instead.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The aggressor is killed for the victim's death.
B) The aggressor is not liable because qisas has been done.
C) The victim's heirs can only claim diyah for the hand.
D) The matter is dropped entirely.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the right to qisas for the soul is separable from the right to the limb, and shubhah can convert the penalty to diyah.

Retaliation and Liability When the Right Is Exceeded
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for excess retaliation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn) on whether a guardian who cuts the killer's hand in retaliation and then pardons is liable for the hand's blood-money, and apply the qiyas and shubha reasoning used.

Core Idea
Retaliation is strictly tied to the right; exceeding it incurs diyah even if pardoned.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute arising from a scenario of multiple injuries during qisas. The matn from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents a case: a guardian cuts the killer's hand in retaliation, then pardons him. Is the guardian liable for the hand? Abu Hanifa says yes, the Sahibayn and al-Shafi'i say no. We will unpack the legal reasoning, including the underlying qiyas and the role of shubha, and see how the rule on sarayah (spread of injury) applies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the legal question
Read the matn: 'ولو قطع يده فاقتص له فمات الأول... وإن قطع يد القاتل، وعفى، ضمن القاطع دية اليد.' The guardian cuts the killer's hand as retaliation and then pardons. The question is whether he must pay diyah for the hand.
Note that the first part of the matn was about sarayah leading to death; here the death does not occur from the cut.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and its proof
Abu Hanifa holds that the guardian is liable for the hand's diyah. His proof: the guardian's right was in killing (the soul), not in cutting a limb. He exceeded his right, so qiyas would require qisas for the cut, but it is dropped due to shubha (doubt because he had some right to take the limb as part of the soul), so diyah becomes due.
Shubha arises from the fact that he was allowed to destroy the whole person, hence doubt about the limb.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and its proof
The Sahibayn (and al-Shafi'i) say no liability. Their proof: the guardian exercised his right by cutting, as he was entitled to destroy the entire life including all its parts. The cut was part of the retaliation, so he is not liable.
They consider the limb as included in the right to take life.
4
Analyze the qiyas and shubha reasoning
Abu Hanifa's qiyas: normally cutting a hand without right would require qisas (hand for hand). But here there is shubha because the guardian had the right to kill, which includes destroying the hand as part of the body. So qisas is dropped, and diyah is obligated instead.
The shubha reduces the severity from qisas to diyah.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical difference: if the guardian is liable, he must pay the full diyah of the hand. If not liable, he pays nothing. This affects the financial burden on the guardian.
The text does not specify a tarjih; later Hanafi works may favor one view.

Book of Homicides. 419. Rulings on multiple injuries. And they have one-third of what he left as a will. And if he cut off his hand and he [the victim] retaliated, and the first [victim] died, he is killed for it. And if he cut off the hand of the killer and pardoned, the cutter is liable for the blood-money of the hand. The guardian of the intentionally killed [victim] — to the extent of the dowry — from the blood-money falls from them. He is the guardian. For the one whose hand is cut due to its spread (sarayah) the like of it, and what exceeds that is considered: if it comes out from the third, it falls from them as a will for them, so it is valid because they are outsiders as he explained by his statement: 'And they have one-third of what he left as a will.' And if it does not come out from the third, the amount of one-third falls and they pay the excess to the guardian because the will has effect only from one-third. (Ayni, Radd al-Muhtar with alteration) 'And they have one-third' etc., meaning from what exceeds the blood-money, i.e., if the blood-money exceeds the fair dowry, the amount of the fair dowry is lifted from the 'aqila, and they have one-third of what exceeds the fair dowry up to the full blood-money as a will, so it falls from them, and they return the surplus to the heirs of the husband if what exceeds the fair dowry does not come from one-third of his wealth, and if it comes from one-third of his wealth, it is also lifted from them, and they do not return anything. The summary is: the fair dowry is either equal to the blood-money, or more, or less. In the first two cases, it all falls from them. In the third, if what exceeds comes out of one-third, it falls from them too; otherwise, one-third of what exceeds falls and they return the surplus. (Compiled from al-Fath and others) 'will': meaning that the 'aqila have one-third of what the deceased left from the blood-money in terms of a will for them, because it was mentioned that the patient's donation in the excess over the fair dowry is a will. And this is a will for the 'aqila because he dropped the blood-money in exchange for the dowry, and the blood-money in homicide by mistake is on the 'aqila, so he has donated to them what exceeds the dowry. 'And if he cut off his hand' etc.: meaning if a man cut off the hand of a man, and he [the victim] retaliated, and the first [person whose hand was cut] died before the second [the one who cut], the second [the cutter] is killed for it, i.e., due to the spread of the cut, because it becomes clear that the injury was intentional murder, and the right of the one entitled to retaliation is in taking life, and executing the cut does not cause his right to kill to fall. From Abu Yusuf: his right to retaliation falls because his proceeding to cut is evidence that he released him from other. And if the one from whom retaliation is taken (i.e., the one whose hand was cut in retaliation) dies from the cut, his blood-money is on the 'aqila of the one entitled to retaliation according to Abu Hanifa; they two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: nothing is upon him because he has exercised his right (the cut), so the ruling of its spread falls. Al-Shafi'i also said this. And the argument for him (Abu Hanifa) is that his right was in cutting, and what occurred was killing, so he did not exercise his right, so he is liable. The analogy would require retaliation, but it was dropped due to shubha, so blood-money is obligated. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/434] 'And pardoned': i.e., then the guardian of the killed pardoned the retaliation from him... 'the cutter is liable' etc.: i.e., the cutter, who is the guardian, is liable for the blood-money of the hand (i.e., the hand of the killer) according to Abu Hanifa, whether the retaliation was decreed for him or not. They two say: nothing is upon him because he exercised his right, so he is not liable; because he was entitled to destroy the life with all its parts; therefore, if he did not pardon, he would not be liable. Similarly, if it spread and did not heal, or he did not pardon and it did not spread, or he cut off his hand and then slit his neck before healing or after. His argument is that he exercised something other than his right, because his right was in killing, and this is cutting and severing. The analogy would require retaliation, but it was dropped due to shubha, since he was entitled to destroy the limb as a follower of the soul. When retaliation drops, blood-money becomes obligatory. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/434]
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Homicides (Kitab al-Jinayat) deals with rulings on multiple injuries (t'adud al-jinaya). It contains a matn from Kanz al-Daqa'iq with commentary from various sources. The passage opens with a ruling: if a person in a marital relationship causes death by wounding, and the blood-money exceeds the fair dowry (mahr al-mithl), the 'aqila (tribal surety) are liable only for the fair dowry; the excess is treated as a will from the deceased, so if it exceeds one-third of his estate, only one-third of the excess is binding. This is because the victim donated the excess to the 'aqila. The commentary explains that if the blood-money equals or exceeds the fair dowry, the entire amount falls on the 'aqila; if less, the excess is subject to will rules. Then the passage discusses a scenario: a man cuts off another's hand; the victim retaliates by cutting the aggressor's hand; the first victim dies before the second. The second (the original cutter) is killed for the death due to the spread of the wound (sarayah), because the injury is deemed intentional. Abu Hanifa holds that the right of retaliation shifts to the soul, and exercising the cut does not forfeit the right to kill. Abu Yusuf argues that the cut implies absolution from further right. If the one who was retaliated against dies from the cut, the blood-money is on the 'aqila of the one who retaliated according to Abu Hanifa, while the Sahibayn say no liability because he exercised his right. Al-Shafi'i follows the Sahibayn. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that the right was to cut, but what occurred was killing, so he did not exercise his right; qiyas would require retaliation but it is dropped due to shubha (doubt), so diyah is due. The next part discusses pardon: if the guardian of the murdered person pardons the killer after retaliation, the guardian is liable for the hand's blood-money according to Abu Hanifa, because he exceeded his right (his right was only to kill, not to mutilate). The Sahibayn say no liability because he was entitled to destroy the entire life, including the limb. The text explains the underlying qiyas and the reason for dropping retaliation due to shubha. The practical differences among the Hanafi imams are highlighted.

Key Terms
السراية (al-sarayah): The spread of an injury from the original wound to cause death or additional harm.
العفو (al-'afw): Pardon by the heir after retaliation, which may affect compensation.
القود (al-qawad): Retaliation in kind (qisas) for homicide or injury.
الشبهة (al-shubhah): Doubt that prevents application of a harsh penalty (hadd or qisas).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling of Abu Hanifa regarding the guardian who cuts the killer's hand in retaliation and then pardons?
A) No liability because he exercised his right.
B) He must pay the diyah of the hand.
C) He must pay one-third of the diyah.
D) He is subject to qisas (hand for hand).

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, retaliation (qisas) is strictly confined to the right; any additional injury beyond the right, even with shubha, results in diyah liability according to Abu Hanifa, while the Sahibayn consider it within the right.

8 min
The Dispute on Re-Establishing Evidence When an Absent Heir Returns
Rulings on testimony when one heir is absent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on re-establishing evidence when an absent heir returns in a qisas case, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of the dispute.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa requires re-establishment of evidence; the Sahibayn deem it unnecessary once the absent heir appears.

In homicide cases where one heir is absent, a procedural dispute arises when the absent heir later returns. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the evidence (bayyinah) must be re-established before qisas can be carried out, as a precaution due to the dual nature of the right (of the deceased and of the heirs). However, his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, hold that re-establishment is unnecessary because the reason for the delay—the possibility of the absent heir's pardon—no longer exists. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
The case involves a homicide with two adult sane guardians (awliya'). One guardian is present, the other absent. The present guardian brings witnesses (bayyinah) against the killer. The killer is imprisoned by consensus, but qisas is not decreed until the absent heir returns.
The text emphasizes that all schools agree on imprisonment and delay of execution.
2
State the Agreed Points
All scholars agree that when the absent heir is absent, the killer is imprisoned and qisas is not carried out because the present heir cannot execute alone. The absent heir's return triggers the dispute.
Consensus (ijma') is cited for these preliminary rulings.
3
Present Abu Hanifa's Position and His Dalil
Abu Hanifa argues that when the absent heir returns, the evidence must be re-established (i.e., the witnesses must testify again) because the right of qisas belongs partly to the deceased and partly to the heirs. Re-establishment is a precaution to ensure all rights are properly considered.
His proof is that the right is dual (haqq al-qatil min wajh wa haqq al-warathah min wajh).
4
Present the Sahibayn's Position and Their Dalil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say re-establishment is unnecessary. The initial prevention was due to the possibility of the absent heir's pardon; once he appears, that possibility is removed, so the original evidence suffices. The three imams (including al-Shafi'i) hold this view.
Their proof: the reason (sabab) for the delay has ceased, so no need to repeat the procedure.
5
Identify the Practical Difference (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
According to Abu Hanifa, if the evidence is not re-established, the qisas cannot proceed. According to the Sahibayn, the original evidence is sufficient, and the killer can be executed immediately upon the absent heir's return. This affects how courts handle the case after the absent heir appears.
The text does not specify which view is mufta bihi, but the dispute is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.

باب الشهادة في القتل
لا يقيد حاضر بحجة إذا أخوه غاب عن خصومة، فإن يعد لابد من إعادته ليقتلا، بإقامة حجة أخو الحاضر الغائب عن الغيبة الغائب البينة عبد الرجلين ولو خطأ أو دينا لا، فإن أثبت القاتل عفو الغائب لم يقتل، وكذا لو قتل عبدهما وأحدهما غائب، وإن شهد وليان بعفو ثالثهما لغت، والحال أن أحدهما شهادتهما وهو عفو عنهما
باب الشهادة إلخ لما كانت الشهادة في القتل أمرا متعلقا بالقتل، أوردها بعد ذكر حكم القتل؛ لأن ما يتعلق بالشيء يكون أدنى درجة ذلك الشيء تكملة البحر الرائق: (۱۱۳/۹] ولأن المقصود من الشهادة إثبات القتل، فكانت غير مقصودة، فناسب أن يذكر حكمها بعد ذكر حكمه. (فتح) لا يقيد إلخ: [من أقاد يقيد أي لا يقتص] يعني إذا قتل رجل، وله وليان بالغان عاقلان، أحدهما حاضر والآخر غائب، فأقام الحاضر بينة على القتل، لا يقتل القاتل قصاصا، فإن عاد الغائب، فليس لهما أن يقتلا بتلك البينة بل لابد لهما من إعادة البينة للقتل عند الإمام، وقالا لا يعيد ،البينة وأجمعوا على أن القاتل يحبس إذا أقام الحاضر البينة؛ لأنه صار متهما بالقتل، والمتهم يحبس، وأجمعوا أيضا على أنه لا يقضى بالقصاص ما لم يحضر الغائب؛ لأن المقصود القصاص، والحاضر لا يتمكن من الاستيفاء بالإجماع تكملة البحر الرائق (۱۱۳/۹] لهما في الخلافية: أن الامتناع لاحتمال العفو، وإذا حضر لم يبق احتمال فلا حاجة إلى الإعادة وبه قالت الثلاثة، وله أنه حق القتيل من وجه وحق الورثة من وجه، فتشترط الإعادة احتياطا رمز الحقائق: ٤٣٥/٢]
يدعي
ولو خطأ إلخ: يعني لو كان القتل خطأ، أو كان الحق الذي ادعاه دينا لأبيهما على آخر، فأقام أحدهما البينة والآخر غائب، ثم حضر، لا يعيد البينة بالإجماع؛ لأنه يمكن من الاستيفاء؛ لأن الخطأ يوجب الدية بطريق ثبوت الوراثة. (ملا مسكين، فتح) لم يقتل: أي لم يقتل القاتل في هذه الصورة بعد حضور الغائب أيضا؛ لأن القاتل على الحاضر سقوط حقه في القود وانتقاله إلى المال ولا يتمكن من إثباته إلا بإثبات العفو من الغائب، فانتصب الحاضر خصما عنه، فإذا قضى عليه صار الغائب مقضيا عليه تبعا له. (فتح، عيني) وكذا لو قتل إلخ: أي لو كان عبد بين رجلين فقتل عمدا أو خطأ، وأحد الموليين غائب، فحكمه مثل ما ذكرنا في أحد الوليين حتى لا يقتل ببينة أقامها الحاضر من غير إعادة بعد عود الغائب، ولو أقام القاتل البينة أن الغائب قد عفا، فالشاهد خصم، ويسقط القصاص لما بينا، فحاصله : أن هذه المسألة مثل الأولى في جميع ما ذكرنا إلا أنه إذا كان القتل عمدا أو خطأ لا يكون الحاضر خصما عن الغائب بالإجماع، والفرق لهما في الكل، ولأبي حنيفة في الخطأ: أن أحد الورثة خصم عن الباقين، ولا كذلك أحد الموليين. (تكملة، فتح)
وإن شهد وليان إلخ: أي إذا كان أولياء المقتول ثلاثة، فشهد اثنان منهم على الثالث أنه عفا، فشهادتهما باطلة؛
Chapter of Testimony in Homicide. The present [heir] is not executed based on evidence if his brother is absent from the lawsuit; then if he returns, they must re-establish the evidence so that both can be executed, by presenting the evidence of the present brother for the absent one [or: the absent brother for the absent one]. [Also] the evidence of the slave of two men [is treated similarly]. And if it is a mistake or a debt, [the rule is] not [the same]. If the killer proves the pardon of the absent [heir], he is not killed. Similarly, if he killed their slave and one of them is absent [the same ruling applies]. And if two guardians testify to the pardon of a third, their testimony is null, and the situation is that one of them [actually] his testimony is a pardon from him. 'Chapter of Testimony' etc.: Since testimony in homicide is a matter related to killing, it is mentioned after the ruling on killing because what relates to a thing is one degree lower than that thing. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 9/113) And because the purpose of testimony is to prove the killing, it is not [the primary] purpose, so it is appropriate to mention its ruling after the ruling on killing. (Fath) 'The present is not executed' etc.: [from 'aqada yuqidu' meaning he does not execute qisas] i.e., if a man is killed and he has two male guardians of sound mind, one present and one absent, and the present one brings evidence of the killing, the killer is not executed in retaliation. Then if the absent one returns, they cannot execute him with that same evidence; rather, they must re-establish the evidence for the killing according to Imam [Abu Hanifa]. They two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: they do not re-establish the evidence. And they all agreed that the killer is imprisoned if the present one brings evidence because he becomes accused of killing, and an accused is imprisoned. They also agreed that qisas is not decreed until the absent one appears because the purpose is qisas, and the present one cannot execute it by consensus. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 9/113) Their argument in al-Khilafiyyat: the prevention is due to the possibility of pardon; when the absent one appears, there is no longer any possibility, so there is no need for re-establishment. And the three imams (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Shafi'i) said this. And his (Abu Hanifa's) argument: it is a right of the killed in one sense and a right of the heirs in another sense, so re-establishment is required as a precaution. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/435) 'And if it is a mistake' etc.: meaning if the killing was by mistake, or the right claimed was a debt owed to their father by another, and one of them brings evidence while the other is absent, then when the absent one appears, the evidence is not re-established by consensus because execution is possible; because mistake causes blood-money by way of inheritance. (Mulla Miskin, Fath) 'He is not killed': i.e., the killer is not killed in this scenario after the absent one's appearance as well, because the killer [demonstrates] against the present one the fall of his right to retaliation and its transfer to property, and he cannot prove it except by proving the pardon of the absent one, so the present one stands as a litigant on his behalf. When judgment is passed against him, the absent one becomes subject to judgment by following him. (Fath, Ayni) 'Similarly, if he killed' etc.: i.e., if a slave belongs to two men and he killed him intentionally or by mistake, and one of the two masters is absent, the ruling is the same as we mentioned for one of the two guardians: he is not killed based on evidence brought by the present one without re-establishment after the absent one returns. And if the killer brings evidence that the absent one has pardoned, then the witness is an opponent, and qisas is dropped as we explained. The summary is that this issue is like the first in everything we mentioned, except that when the killing is intentional or by mistake, the present one is not a litigant on behalf of the absent one by consensus; there is a difference [between guardians and masters] in all cases. According to Abu Hanifa in mistake: one of the heirs is a litigant on behalf of the rest, but not one of the two masters. (Takmila, Fath) 'And if two guardians testify' etc.: i.e., if there are three guardians of the killed person, and two of them testify that the third has pardoned, their testimony is null.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the 'Chapter of Testimony in Homicide' from Kanz al-Daqa'iq with commentary. The author justifies placing testimony after the ruling on killing because testimony is a means to an end. The central ruling: if a homicide has two adult sane guardians (awliya'), one present and one absent, the present guardian's testimony alone is not sufficient for execution (qisas). When the absent guardian returns, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, they must re-present the evidence before both can execute; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani say they need not re-establish the evidence because the reason for delay (possibility of pardon) is gone. All agree the killer is imprisoned during absence and qisas is not decreed until the absent one appears. The commentary then distinguishes cases of accidental killing or debt claims, where re-establishment is not needed by consensus because the remedy (blood-money) can be shared directly. If the killer proves that the absent guardian has pardoned, qisas is dropped because the present guardian becomes a litigant for the absent one. The same rule applies to a slave jointly owned by two masters, but with a difference: in homicide (intentional or accidental), the present master cannot represent the absent master by consensus, while in inheritance, one heir can represent others. Finally, if two out of three guardians testify that the third has pardoned, their testimony is invalid because they are testifying in their own interest (since the pardon would benefit them by reducing their share or risk of retaliation). The passage covers key procedural intricacies of qisas when heirs are absent.

Key Terms
البينة (al-bayyinah): Legal evidence or proof, typically through witness testimony.
الولي (al-wali): Guardian or heir entitled to demand qisas or diyah in homicide cases.
الغائب (al-gha'ib): The absent heir who is not present at the time of testimony or execution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, when an absent heir returns after a present heir has given testimony in a qisas case, what must happen?
A) The killer is executed immediately with the existing evidence.
B) The evidence must be re-established before both heirs can execute.
C) The case is dismissed and the killer is set free.
D) The absent heir must give new testimony alone.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa requires re-establishment of evidence when an absent heir returns due to the dual nature of the right; the Sahibayn deem it unnecessary as the reason for delay has vanished.
